Al-Bukhari's Proof of Mutual Prevention
Al-Bukhari provides a detailed rational proof of tawhid using indivisible atoms and impossibility of two equal powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Bukhari's detailed formulation of burhan al-tamanu' and how it uses the indivisible atom to demonstrate the impossibility of two equal divine powers.

Core Idea
Two equal powers cannot coexist—they would be incapable of creating anything.

Al-Bukhari presents a rigorous rational argument for tawhid by focusing on an indivisible atom. He shows that if two beings possessed equal power, they would be unable to create anything, contradicting our observation of the world. This passage unpacks that logical proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow and the key terms introduced.
The proof is dense; focus on the main structure first.
2
Identify the core assumption
The argument begins by assuming a being with power equal to Allah's over all possible things. Understand why this assumption leads to a contradiction.
3
Focus on the indivisible atom
Al-Bukhari narrows the discussion to an indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) to avoid complications of divisible effects. See how this simplification strengthens the proof.
This atom is the smallest unit that cannot be split.
4
Analyze the two logical possibilities
If both powers act on the atom, either the atom is created by both (resulting in tahsil al-hasil) or by one alone (making the other incapable). Trace these implications and conclude that both powers must be incapable.
Tahsil al-hasil means obtaining what is already obtained—a logical redundancy.
5
Connect to the Quranic verse
Al-Bukhari ends by referencing the verse 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted.' Reflect on how the rational proof supports the revealed text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نسخة أخرى: قاهراً، ويسمى هذا البرهان عند القوم برهان التمانع وقد إختلفت عبارات القوم في تقرير هذا البرهان بعبارات مختلفة فقال صاحب الإملاء علي البخاري ما نصه: أحدهما إنه قام البرهان القاطع على وجوب عموم قدرته وإرادته لجميع الممكنات، فلو نذر موجود له من القدرة على إيجاد ممكن ما مثل ماله تعالى لزم عند تعلق تينك القدرتين أن لا يوجد شيء من العالم بهما لما يلزم عليه من تحصيل الحاصل أو كون الأثر الواحد أثرين، لأن المسألة مفروضة فيما لا ينقسم كالجوهر الفرد فلا بد من عجزهما إن لم يوجد بهما، ومن عجز إن وجد بأحدهما دون الآخر، ويلزم من عجز أحدهما عجز الآخر، لأنه مثله، عجزهما في هذا الممكن لزم عجزهما في سائر الممكنات إذ لا فرق، وذلك يستلزم إستحالة وجود الحوادث وهو محال، لأنه خلاف الحس والعيان؛ وإذا إستبان وجوب عجزهما مع الإتفاق فمع الإختلاف أبين وإليه الإشارة بالآية الكريمة وهي قوله تعالى: لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا) اهـ
And in another copy: 'subjugating' (qahiran), and this proof is called by the people (al-qawm) the proof of mutual prevention (burhan al-tamanu'), and the expressions of the people have differed in formulating this proof with different phrases. So the author of al-Imla' 'Ali al-Bukhari said, its text: One of them is that the decisive proof has been established for the necessity of the universality of His power and will over all possible things. So if there were assumed a being having the power to bring into existence a certain possible thing like His power, the Exalted, then necessarily, when these two powers are engaged, nothing of the world would come into existence by them due to the necessary consequence of obtaining the already obtained (tahsil al-hasil) or a single effect having two causes, because the issue is considered in what is indivisible like the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). So either both of them must be incapable if [the effect] is not brought about by them, or one is incapable if it is brought about by one without the other. And from the incapacity of one follows the incapacity of the other, because he is like him. Their incapacity in this possible implies their incapacity in all possible things, since there is no distinction. And that necessitates the impossibility of the occurrence of events, which is impossible because it contradicts sense and observation. And if the necessity of their incapacity is evident in agreement, then in disagreement it is more evident. And to that the noble verse refers, His statement: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted.' End.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage expands on the proof of tawhid (burhan al-tamanu') as formulated by the scholar al-Bukhari. It begins by noting a textual variant and then explains the proof in detail. The argument assumes two beings with power equal to God's own over all possible things. It focuses on a single indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) to avoid the complication of divisible effects. If both powers will the same thing regarding this atom, either the atom is created by both (making each effect redundant, which is 'tahsil al-hasil' or obtaining what is already obtained) or by one alone (making the other's will ineffective). If the atom is not created by either, both are incapable; if created by one only, the other is incapable. Since the two powers are assumed equal, the incapacity of one implies the incapacity of the other. Consequently, both would be incapable of acting on any possible thing, which is impossible because we observe events coming into existence. Therefore, the assumption of two equal divine powers is false. This logical argument is the core of burhan al-tamanu'.

Key Terms
برهان التمانع
تحصيل الحاصل
الجوهر الفرد
عموم القدرة
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of mutual prevention only works if the two gods disagree with each other.
Correction: Al-Bukhari shows that even if they agree, the proof holds because a single effect cannot have two equal causes without redundancy or incapacity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bukhari's proof, what is the logical conclusion if two equal powers are assumed to act on an indivisible atom?
A) Both powers must be capable and the atom is created
B) Both powers must be incapable, which is impossible
C) One power overcomes the other
D) The atom is created by one and destroyed by the other

Key Takeaway
Al-Bukhari's proof demonstrates that the existence of multiple divine powers is rationally impossible because they would be unable to produce any effect, contradicting the observed occurrence of events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever seriously pondered the logical impossibility of multiple gods, or do you simply accept Tawhid as inherited culture? If you were asked to defend it rationally, could you explain this proof from your own understanding?
?
The proof shows that two equal powers lead to chaos and inaction. In your own life, where are you trying to serve two masters—like pleasing people while obeying Allah? Will you now cut out divided loyalties based on this truth?
?
If this proof demonstrates that only One Power can truly act, why do you still fear the disapproval of others or rely on your own strength? How will you shift your trust entirely to the One whose power is universal?

Ibn al-Qushayri's Proof of Mutual Prevention
Ibn al-Qushayri presents the same proof by considering two makers and the impossibility of contradictory wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Qushayri's logical argument for the oneness of God based on the impossibility of contradictory wills and the requirement of divine perfection.

Core Idea
Two fully powerful gods would create an impossible contradiction; thus only one God can exist.

Ibn al-Qushayri presents a rigorous logical proof for tawhid based on the impossibility of two fully powerful beings having conflicting wills. By examining all scenarios—both powerful, both weak, or one weak—he shows that only one divine maker is rationally possible. This passage expands on the classic burhan al-tamanu'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation once to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'if there were two makers'.
2
Identify the three cases examined
Ibn al-Qushayri considers three possibilities: both makers are fully powerful, both are incapable, or one is incapable. Trace each case through the passage.
The proof systematically eliminates each scenario except the one true God.
3
Understand the contradiction in the first case
If both are fully powerful and have conflicting wills, implementing both wills would make a single body both existent and non-existent at the same time—a logical impossibility. Therefore, this case is impossible.
This is the core of burhan al-tamanu'.
4
Analyze the second and third cases
If one or both makers are incapable, then they cannot be divine because divinity requires eternal power. Incapacity is meaningless in an eternal being since it presupposes an impossible action.
Thus, any second maker is either a source of contradiction or not divine.
5
Conclude the argument
Having eliminated all alternatives, the only rational conclusion is that there is exactly one eternal, all-powerful Creator. This is the essence of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن القشيري رضي الله عنه: وإذا لزم الدليل على وحدانيته تعالى أنه لو كان للعالم صانعان فصاعداً لم يخل: أما أن يكونا قادرين، فلو كانا قادرين على الكمال لجاز في العقول تمانعهما بأن يريد أحدهما بقاء الجسم في حالة معينة ويريد الآخر فناءه في تلك الحالة فإذا قدرا على تنفيذ إرادتهما أدى ذلك إلى المحال وهو أن يكون الجسم الواحد موجوداً معدوماً في حالة واحدة، وما أدى إلى المحال محال، وإن كانا عاجزين أو كان أحدهما عاجزاً فالعاجز لا يصلح للالإلهية لأن الصانع قديم وعجز قديم محال لأن العجز لا يكون إلا عن فعل يعجز عنه وما لم يتصور الفعل لم يتصور العجز، وتقدير الفعل في الأزل محال؛ وإن لم يكونا قادرين على الكمال فليفرض الدليل في أن يريد أحدهما وجود جوهر ويريد الآخر أن لا يوجد هذا إذا لم يقدر أحدهما على شيء من الأعراض، فلنفرض الدليل في أن يريد الآخر ضده ويذكر الدليل بأسره. اهـ
And Ibn al-Qushayri, may Allah be pleased with him, said: And when the proof necessitates His oneness, the Exalted, that if there were two or more makers of the world, it would be inevitable: either they are both powerful. So if they were both powerful to perfection, their mutual prevention would be possible in intellects, in that one of them intends the continuation of a body in a certain state and the other intends its annihilation in that state. So if they were capable of implementing their wills, that would lead to the impossible, namely that one body would be existent and non-existent in one state. And whatever leads to the impossible is impossible. And if they are both incapable or one of them is incapable, then the incapable is not fit for divinity because the maker is eternal, and eternal incapacity is impossible, because incapacity is only [conceived] with respect to an action that one is incapable of, and as long as the action is not conceived, incapacity is not conceived. And positing an action in pre-eternity is impossible. And if they are not both powerful to perfection, then let the proof be posited in that one of them intends the existence of a substance and the other intends that it not exist, if one of them has no power over any accident. Then let the proof be posited in that the other intends its opposite, and the proof is fully mentioned. End.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage gives another formulation of burhan al-tamanu' by Ibn al-Qushayri. The proof begins by assuming two or more makers of the world. They are either both fully powerful or not. If both are fully powerful, it is logically possible for them to have conflicting wills: one wants a body to remain, the other wants it to perish. If both were able to carry out their wills, the same body would have to be both existent and non-existent at the same time, which is a logical impossibility. Therefore, the assumption of two fully powerful gods is impossible. If, on the other hand, one or both are incapable, then the incapable being cannot be divine because divinity requires eternal power; eternal incapacity is meaningless since incapacity relates to an action that cannot be performed, and positing an action in pre-eternity is absurd. So the only remaining possibility is that there is exactly one God. This proof covers all logical options and reinforces the necessity of tawhid.

Key Terms
الصانع
التمانع
المحال
القديم
الجوهر
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof only works if the two gods are equally powerful, but Ibn al-Qushayri also addresses cases where one is less powerful.
Correction: The proof covers all possibilities: both powerful, both weak, or one weak. In each case, multiple gods are impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Qushayri, what is the logical consequence if two fully powerful gods intend opposite states for the same body?
A) The body will exist in two states simultaneously
B) The body will be both existent and non-existent at the same time
C) One god will submit to the other
D) The body will be annihilated

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Qushayri's proof shows that any assumption of multiple divine creators leads to logical impossibility, affirming that God is one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that any being less than all-powerful cannot be God. Are there aspects of your life where you are implicitly trusting a 'lesser god'—like your own strength, a person, or a system—rather than the One all-powerful Creator? What will you do today to shift that trust back to Him?
?
If the assumption of two gods leads to logical impossibility, why do you sometimes act as if there are competing forces controlling your destiny (e.g., fate versus free will, or worldly distractions versus divine command)? What does this reveal about your tawhid, and what concrete step can you take to reinforce His sole control?
?
Ibn al-Qushayri argues that eternal incapacity is incoherent. Where in your life are you content to remain in a state of spiritual incapacity, thinking 'I can't change' or 'I'm too weak'? How does knowing that God is all-powerful and that your weakness is temporary (not eternal) motivate you to seek His help to overcome that excuse?

Allah's Existence
intermediate

Proof of the Impossibility of Two Gods
Ghazali argues that two gods with conflicting wills leads to absurdity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that the coexistence of two gods leads to absurdity due to conflicting wills.

Core Idea
Two gods with opposite wills cannot both be God — absurdity proves unity.

This short but dense passage from Ghazali presents a cornerstone of Islamic theology: the impossibility of two gods. Using a simple logical scenario — two beings willing opposite things for the same object — he demonstrates that the assumption of two gods leads to a contradiction. The argument is crisp: if both wills are executed, the same thing would exist and not exist, which is impossible. If one or both are incapable, that is also impossible for a divine being. Through this, Ghazali establishes divine unity as a logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: the scenario, the absurd consequence, and the alternative cases.
The passage uses terms like 'محال' (absurd) and 'عجز' (incapacity).
2
Identify the main argument
The core argument is: if two gods have opposite wills, either both wills are executed (leading to absurdity), or one or both are incapable. Both outcomes eliminate the possibility of two gods. Identify these two branches.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the concept of 'eternal incapacity'
Ghazali argues that incapacity cannot be eternal because incapacity requires an act that is conceived. An act in eternity is not conceivable. This supports the idea that an eternal being cannot be incapable.
This is a subtle point about the nature of God.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Read the common misunderstanding. Why is it not enough for two gods to simply never disagree? Because the potential for conflict, even if not actual, would still entail logical problems. Discuss this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويريد الآخر فناءه في تلك الحالة فإذا قدر أعلى تنفيذ إرادتهما أدى ذلك إلى المحال وهو أن يكون الجسم الواحد موجوداً معدوماً في حالة واحدة ، وما أدى إلى المحال محال ، وإن كانا عاجزين أو كان أحدهما عاجزاً فالعاجز لا يصلح للالهية لأن الصانع قديم وعجز قديم محال لأن العجز لا يكون إلا عن فعل يعجز عنه وما لم يتصور الفعل لم يتصور العجز ، وتقدير الفعل في الأزل محال ؛ وإن لم يكونا قادرين على الكمال فليفرض الدليل في أن يريد أحدهما وجود جوهر ويريد الآخر أن لا يوجد هذا إذا لم يقدر أحدهما على شيء من الأعراض ، فلنفرض الدليل في أن يريد الآخر ضده ويذكر الدليل بأسره . اهـ
And the other wills its annihilation in that state. If both wills are assumed to be executed, that leads to absurdity — namely, that one body exists and does not exist in the same state. And whatever leads to absurdity is absurd. If both are incapable, or one of them is incapable, then the incapable one is not fit for divinity, because the Maker is eternal and an eternal incapacity is absurd, for incapacity only exists regarding an act that one is incapable of, and if the act is not conceivable, then incapacity is not conceivable, and positing an act in eternity is absurd. And if both are not perfectly powerful, then let the proof be assumed: that one wills the existence of a substance, and the other wills that it not exist — this if neither has power over any accidents. Then let us assume the proof that the other wills its opposite, and mention the proof in its entirety.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents a theological proof for the impossibility of two gods. It is an argument based on conflicting wills. The author (Imam Ghazali) begins by considering two beings, each having a will: one wills the existence of a body, the other wills its annihilation in the same state. If both wills are executed, the same body would simultaneously exist and not exist, which is an absurdity (المحال). Since whatever leads to absurdity is itself absurd (محال), the scenario of two such wills being realized is impossible. The author then considers the possibility that one or both are incapable of executing their will. If a being is incapable, it cannot be divine because the Maker (الصانع) is eternal and eternal incapacity is absurd. Incapacity only makes sense when there is an act that one is unable to perform; if the act itself cannot be conceived (like an act in eternity), then incapacity is also inconceivable. Finally, the argument turns to a case where the two beings are not perfectly powerful: one wills the existence of a substance, and the other wills its non-existence, assuming neither has power over accidents. The proof is to be presented in full, but here it is summarized: the conflict of wills leads to absurdity, thus establishing the impossibility of two gods.

Key Terms
إرادة
محال
عجز
إلهية
قديم
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could coexist if they always agree and never have conflicting wills.
Correction: Ghazali's argument demonstrates that even the hypothetical possibility of opposite wills (which must exist if each is omnipotent and independent) leads to absurdity, proving that two gods cannot exist regardless of agreement.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, what absurd consequence follows if two gods will opposite things?
A) The weaker god would lose power.
B) The same body would both exist and not exist in the same state.
C) The universe would be destroyed.
D) The gods would fight each other.

Key Takeaway
Islamic theology affirms the unity of God through logical necessity: the assumption of two gods leads to absurdity, impossibility, or incapacity, none of which can be attributed to the Divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself mentally serving two masters—wanting God's approval but also craving the world's praise? How does this logical proof that only one God can truly exist expose the absurdity of divided loyalty? What concrete step will you take today to align your heart with that single allegiance?
?
When you face doubts about God's unity, do you dismiss rational proofs like this one as 'too philosophical'? Or are you willing to examine your own assumptions? Think of a time you felt torn between competing desires; in light of this lesson, how would you argue to yourself that only one ultimate Authority deserves your submission?
